185,000\n        </td>\n        <td align="right">\n         180.1\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       <i>\n        Rocket thrusts are vacuum thrusts unless otherwise noted\n       </i>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Of the liquid propellants used, density is worst for\n       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">\n        liquid hydrogen\n       </a>\n       . Although this propellant is marvellous in many ways, it has a very low density, about one fourteenth that of water. This makes the turbopumps and pipework larger and heavier, and this is reflected in the thrust-to-weight ratio of engines that use it (for example the SSME) compared to those that do not (NK-33).\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cooling">\n        Cooling\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cooling">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       For efficiency reasons, and because they physically can, rockets run with combustion temperatures that can reach ~3,500 K (~3,200\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C or ~5,800\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0F).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Most other jet engines have gas turbines in the hot exhaust. Due to their larger surface area, they are harder to cool and hence there is a need to run the combustion processes at much lower temperatures, losing efficiency. In addition,\n       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/duct_engine" title="wiktionary:duct engine">\n        duct engines\n       </a>\n       use air as an oxidant, which contains 78% largely unreactive nitrogen, which dilutes the reaction and lowers the temperatures.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutton_6-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sutton-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Rockets have none of these inherent disadvantages.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Therefore, temperatures used in rockets are very often far higher than the melting point of the nozzle and combustion chamber materials (~1,200 K for copper). Two exceptions are\n       <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">\n        graphite\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">\n        tungsten\n       </a>\n       , although both are subject to oxidation if not protected. Indeed, many construction materials can make perfectly acceptable propellants in their own right. It is important that these materials be prevented from combusting, melting or vaporising to the point of failure. This is sometimes somewhat facetiously termed an "engine-rich exhaust". Materials technology could potentially place an upper limit on the exhaust temperature of chemical rockets.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Alternatively, rockets may use more common construction materials such as aluminium, steel, nickel or copper alloys and employ cooling systems that prevent the construction material itself becoming too hot.\n       <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_cooling_(rocket)" title="Regenerative cooling (rocket)">\n        Regenerative cooling\n       </a>\n       , where the propellant is passed through tubes around the combustion chamber or nozzle, and other techniques, such as curtain cooling or film cooling, are employed to give longer nozzle and chamber life. These techniques ensure that a gaseous thermal\n       <a href="/wiki/Boundary_layer" title="Boundary layer">\n        boundary layer\n       </a>\n       touching the material is kept below the temperature which would cause the material to catastrophically fail.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In rockets, the heat fluxes that can pass through the wall are among the highest in engineering; fluxes are generally in the range of 1-200 MW/m\n       <sup>\n        2\n       </sup>\n       . The strongest heat fluxes are found at the throat, which often sees twice that found in the associated chamber and nozzle. This is due to the combination of high speeds (which gives a very thin boundary layer), and although lower than the chamber, the high temperatures seen there. (See\n       <a href="#Rocket_nozzles">\n        rocket nozzles\n       </a>\n       above for temperatures in nozzle).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In rockets the coolant methods include:\n      </p>\n      <ol>\n       <li>\n        uncooled (used for short runs mainly during testing)\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Ablation" title="Ablation">\n         ablative\n        </a>\n        walls (walls are lined with a material that is continuously vaporised and carried away).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Radiative_cooling" title="Radiative cooling">\n         radiative cooling\n        </a>\n        (the chamber becomes almost white hot and radiates the heat away)\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        dump cooling (a propellant, usually\n        <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">\n         hydrogen\n        </a>\n        , is passed around the chamber and dumped)\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_cooling_(rocket)" title="Regenerative cooling (rocket)">\n         regenerative cooling\n        </a>\n        (\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_rocket" title="Liquid rocket">\n         liquid rockets\n        </a>\n        use the fuel, or occasionally the oxidiser, to cool the chamber via a cooling jacket before being injected)\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        curtain cooling (propellant injection is arranged so the temperature of the gases is cooler at the walls)\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        film cooling (surfaces are wetted with liquid propellant, which cools as it evaporates)\n       </li>\n      </ol>\n      <p>\n       In all cases the cooling effect that prevents the wall from being destroyed is caused by a thin layer of insulating fluid (a\n       <a href="/wiki/Boundary_layer" title="Boundary layer">\n        boundary layer\n       </a>\n       ) that is in contact with the walls that is far cooler than the combustion temperature. Provided this boundary layer is intact the wall will not be damaged.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Disruption of the boundary layer may occur during cooling failures or combustion instabilities, and wall failure typically occurs soon after.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       With regenerative cooling a second boundary layer is found in the coolant channels around the chamber. This boundary layer thickness needs to be as small as possible, since the boundary layer acts as an insulator between the wall and the coolant. This may be achieved by making the coolant\n       <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">\n        velocity\n       </a>\n       in the channels as high as possible.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In practice, regenerative cooling is nearly always used in conjunction with curtain cooling and/or film cooling.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Liquid-fuelled engines are often run\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air-fuel_ratio" title="Air-fuel ratio">\n        fuel-rich\n       </a>\n       , which lowers combustion temperatures. This reduces heat loads on the engine and allows lower cost materials and a simplified cooling system. This can also\n       <i>\n        increase\n       </i>\n       performance by lowering the average molecular weight of the exhaust and increasing the efficiency with which combustion heat is converted to kinetic exhaust energy.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_issues">\n        Mechanical issues\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mechanical issues">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       Rocket combustion chambers are normally operated at fairly high pressure, typically 10-200 bar (1 to 20 MPa, 150-3,000 psi). When operated within significant atmospheric pressure, higher combustion chamber pressures give better performance by permitting a larger and more efficient nozzle to be fitted without it being grossly overexpanded.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       However, these high pressures cause the outermost part of the chamber to be under very large\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoop_stress" title="Hoop stress">\n        hoop stresses\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x93 rocket engines are\n       <a href="/wiki/Pressure_vessel" title="Pressure vessel">\n        pressure vessels\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Worse, due to the high temperatures created in rocket engines the materials used tend to have a significantly lowered working tensile strength.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In addition, significant temperature gradients are set up in the walls of the chamber and nozzle, these cause differential expansion of the inner liner that create\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_stresses" title="Internal stresses">\n        internal stresses\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustic_issues">\n        Acoustic issues\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Acoustic issues">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       The extreme vibration and acoustic environment inside a rocket motor commonly result in peak stresses well above mean values, especially in the presence of\n       <a href="/wiki/Organ_pipe" title="Organ pipe">\n        organ pipe\n       </a>\n       -like resonances and gas turbulence.\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion_instabilities">\n        Combustion instabilities\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Combustion instabilities">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The combustion may display undesired instabilities, of sudden or periodic nature. The pressure in the injection chamber may increase until the propellant flow through the injector plate decreases; a moment later the pressure drops and the flow increases, injecting more propellant in the combustion chamber which burns a moment later, and again increases the chamber pressure, repeating the cycle. This may lead to high-amplitude pressure oscillations, often in ultrasonic range, which may damage the motor. Oscillations of \xc2\xb1200\xc2\xa0psi at 25\xc2\xa0kHz were the cause of failures of early versions of the\n       <a href="/wiki/LGM-25C_Titan_II" title="LGM-25C Titan II">\n        Titan II\n       </a>\n       missile second stage engines. The other failure mode is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Deflagration_to_detonation_transition" title="Deflagration to detonation transition">\n        deflagration to detonation transition\n       </a>\n       ; the supersonic\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_wave" title="Pressure wave">\n        pressure wave\n       </a>\n       formed in the combustion chamber may destroy the engine.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-titan2_22-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-titan2-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Combustion instability was also a problem during\n       <a href="/wiki/SM-65_Atlas" title="SM-65 Atlas">\n        Atlas\n       </a>\n       development. The Rocketdyne engines used in the Atlas family were found to suffer from this effect in several static firing tests, and three missile launches exploded on the pad due to rough combustion in the booster engines. In most cases, it occurred while attempting to start the engines with a "dry start" method whereby the igniter mechanism would be activated prior to propellant injection. During the process of man-rating Atlas for\n       <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">\n        Project Mercury\n       </a>\n       , solving combustion instability was a high priority, and the final two Mercury flights sported an upgraded propulsion system with baffled injectors and a hypergolic igniter.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The problem affecting Atlas vehicles was mainly the so-called "racetrack" phenomenon, where burning propellant would swirl around in a circle at faster and faster speeds, eventually producing vibration strong enough to rupture the engine, leading to complete destruction of the rocket. It was eventually solved by adding several baffles around the injector face to break up swirling propellant.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       More significantly, combustion instability was a problem with the Saturn F-1 engines. Some of the early units tested exploded during static firing, which led to the addition of injector baffles.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In the Soviet space program, combustion instability also proved a problem on some rocket engines, including the RD-107 engine used in the R-7 family and the RD-216 used in the R-14 family, and several failures of these vehicles occurred before the problem was solved. Soviet engineering and manufacturing processes never satisfactorily resolved combustion instability in larger RP-1/LOX engines, so the RD-171 engine used to power the Zenit family still used four smaller thrust chambers fed by a common engine mechanism.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The combustion instabilities can be provoked by remains of cleaning solvents in the engine (e.g. the first attempted launch of a Titan II in 1962), reflected shock wave, initial instability after ignition, explosion near the nozzle that reflects into the combustion chamber, and many more factors. In stable engine designs the oscillations are quickly suppressed; in unstable designs they persist for prolonged periods. Oscillation suppressors are commonly used.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Periodic variations of thrust, caused by combustion instability or longitudinal vibrations of structures between the tanks and the engines which modulate the propellant flow, are known as "\n       <a href="/wiki/Pogo_oscillation" title="Pogo oscillation">\n        pogo oscillations\n       </a>\n       " or "pogo", named after the\n       <a href="/wiki/Pogo_stick" title="Pogo stick">\n        pogo stick\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Three different types of combustion instabilities occur:\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chugging">\n        Chugging\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Chugging">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       This is a low frequency oscillation at a few Hertz in chamber pressure usually caused by pressure variations in feed lines due to variations in acceleration of the vehicle. This can cause cyclic variation in thrust, and the effects can vary from merely annoying to actually damaging the payload or vehicle. Chugging can be minimised by using gas-filled damping tubes on feed lines of high density propellants.\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Buzzing">\n        Buzzing\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Buzzing">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       This can be caused due to insufficient pressure drop across the injectors. It generally is mostly annoying, rather than being damaging. However, in extreme cases combustion can end up being forced backwards through the injectors \xe2\x80\x93 this can cause explosions with monopropellants.\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Screeching">\n        Screeching\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Screeching">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       This is the most immediately damaging, and the hardest to control. It is due to acoustics within the combustion chamber that often couples to the chemical combustion processes that are the primary drivers of the energy release, and can lead to unstable resonant "screeching" that commonly leads to catastrophic failure due to thinning of the insulating thermal boundary layer. Acoustic oscillations can be excited by thermal processes, such as the flow of hot air through a pipe or combustion in a chamber. Specifically, standing acoustic waves inside a chamber can be intensified if combustion occurs more intensely in regions where the pressure of the acoustic wave is maximal.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">\n        <a href="#cite_note-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">\n        <a href="#cite_note-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">\n        <a href="#cite_note-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Such effects are very difficult to predict analytically during the design process, and have usually been addressed by expensive, time consuming and extensive testing, combined with trial and error remedial correction measures.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Screeching is often dealt with by detailed changes to injectors, or changes in the propellant chemistry, or vaporising the propellant before injection, or use of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helmholtz_damper" title="Helmholtz damper">\n        Helmholtz dampers\n       </a>\n       within the combustion chambers to change the resonant modes of the chamber.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Testing for the possibility of screeching is sometimes done by exploding small explosive charges outside the combustion chamber with a tube set tangentially to the combustion chamber near the injectors to determine the engine\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Impulse_response" title="Impulse response">\n        impulse response\n       </a>\n       and then evaluating the time response of the chamber pressure- a fast recovery indicates a stable system.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exhaust_noise">\n        Exhaust noise\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Exhaust noise">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_signature" title="Acoustic signature">\n        acoustic signature\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       For all but the very smallest sizes, rocket exhaust compared to other engines is generally very noisy. As the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypersonic" title="Hypersonic">\n        hypersonic\n       </a>\n       exhaust mixes with the ambient air,\n       <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">\n        shock waves\n       </a>\n       are formed. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">\n        Space Shuttle\n       </a>\n       generates over 200\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DB(A)" title="DB(A)">\n        dB(A)\n       </a>\n       of noise around its base. To reduce this, and the risk of payload damage or injury to the crew atop the stack, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Launcher_Platform" title="Mobile Launcher Platform">\n        Mobile Launcher Platform\n       </a>\n       was fitted with a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_Suppression_System" title="Sound Suppression System">\n        Sound Suppression System\n       </a>\n       that sprayed 1,100,000\xc2\xa0litres of water around the base of the rocket in 41 seconds at launch time. Using this system kept sound levels within the payload bay to 142\xc2\xa0dB\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Sound_intensity" title="Sound intensity">\n        sound intensity\n       </a>\n       from the shock waves generated depends on the size of the rocket and on the exhaust velocity. Such shock waves seem to account for the characteristic crackling and popping sounds produced by large rocket engines when heard live. These noise peaks typically overload microphones and audio electronics, and so are generally weakened or entirely absent in recorded or broadcast audio reproductions. For large rockets at close range, the acoustic effects could actually kill.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CR566_28-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CR566-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       More worryingly for space agencies, such sound levels can also damage the launch structure, or worse, be reflected back at the comparatively delicate rocket above. This is why so much water is typically used at launches. The water spray changes the acoustic qualities of the air and reduces or deflects the sound energy away from the rocket.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Generally speaking, noise is most intense when a rocket is close to the ground, since the noise from the engines radiates up away from the jet, as well as reflecting off the ground. Also, when the vehicle is moving slowly, little of the chemical energy input to the engine can go into increasing the kinetic energy of the rocket (since useful power\n       <i>\n        P\n       </i>\n       transmitted to the vehicle is\n       <span class="mwe-math-element">\n        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n          <semantics>\n           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n             <mi>\n              P\n             </mi>\n             <mo>\n              =\n             </mo>\n             <mi>\n              F\n             </mi>\n             <mo>\n              \xe2\x88\x97\n              <!-- \xe2\x88\x97 -->\n             </mo>\n             <mi>\n              V\n             </mi>\n            </mstyle>\n           </mrow>\n           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n            {\\displaystyle P=F*V}\n           </annotation>\n          </semantics>\n         </math>\n        </span>\n        <img alt="P=F*V" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5a72ed128e15e273b95f5b76e790341c6ee8b803" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:10.619ex; height:2.176ex;"/>\n       </span>\n       for thrust\n       <i>\n        F\n       </i>\n       and speed\n       <i>\n        V\n       </i>\n       ). Then the largest portion of the energy is dissipated in the exhaust\'s interaction with the ambient air, producing noise. This noise can be reduced somewhat by flame trenches with roofs, by water injection around the jet and by deflecting the jet at an angle.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">\n        Testing\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Testing">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       Rocket engines are usually statically tested at a\n       <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_test_facility" title="Rocket engine test facility">\n        test facility\n       </a>\n       before being put into production. For high altitude engines, either a shorter nozzle must be used, or the rocket must be tested in a large vacuum chamber.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">\n        Safety\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Safety">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">\n        Rocket\n       </a>\n       vehicles have a reputation for unreliability and danger; especially catastrophic failures. Contrary to this reputation, carefully designed rockets can be made arbitrarily reliable.\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n       In military use, rockets are not unreliable. However, one of the main non-military uses of rockets is for orbital launch. In this application, the premium has typically been placed on minimum weight, and it is difficult to achieve high reliability and low weight simultaneously. In addition, if the number of flights launched is low, there is a very high chance of a design, operations or manufacturing error causing destruction of the vehicle.\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n       Essentially all launch vehicles are test vehicles by normal aerospace standards (as of 2016\n       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">\n        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit">\n         [update]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ).\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">\n          <span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (January 2017)">\n           original research?\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Saturn_family_(1961-1975)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_family_.281961-1975.29">\n        Saturn family (1961-1975)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Saturn family (1961-1975)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_H-1" title="Rocketdyne H-1">\n        Rocketdyne H-1\n       </a>\n       engine, used in a cluster of eight in the first stage of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_I" title="Saturn I">\n        Saturn I\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">\n        Saturn IB\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">\n        launch vehicles\n       </a>\n       , had no catastrophic failures in 152 engine-flights. The\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratt_and_Whitney" title="Pratt and Whitney">\n        Pratt and Whitney\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/RL10" title="RL10">\n        RL10\n       </a>\n       engine, used in a cluster of six in the Saturn I second stage, had no catastrophic failures in 36 engine-flights. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1" title="Rocketdyne F-1">\n        Rocketdyne F-1\n       </a>\n       engine, used in a cluster of five in the first stage of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">\n        Saturn V\n       </a>\n       , had no failures in 65 engine-flights. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_J-2" title="Rocketdyne J-2">\n        Rocketdyne J-2\n       </a>\n       engine, used in a cluster of five in the Saturn V second stage, and singly in the Saturn IB second stage and Saturn V third stage, had no catastrophic failures in 86 engine-flights.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n        <a href="#cite_note-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Space_Shuttle_(1981-2011)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Shuttle_.281981-2011.29">\n        Space Shuttle (1981-2011)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Space Shuttle (1981-2011)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">\n        Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster\n       </a>\n       , used in pairs, caused\n       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">\n        one notable catastrophic failure\n       </a>\n       in 270 engine-flights.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Main_Engine" title="Space Shuttle Main Engine">\n        Space Shuttle Main Engine\n       </a>\n       , used in a cluster of three, flew in 46 refurbished engine units. These made a total of 405 engine-flights with no catastrophic in-flight failures. A single in-flight SSME failure occurring during\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">\n         Space Shuttle\n        </a>\n       </span>\n       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">\n        <i>\n         Challenger\n        </i>\n       </a>\n       \'s\n       <a href="/wiki/STS-51-F" title="STS-51-F">\n        STS-51-F\n       </a>\n       mission,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P.26WFS_30-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-P.26WFS-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       resulting in a 99.95% reliability rate.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P.26WChart_31-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-P.26WChart-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This failure had no effect on mission objectives or duration.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hale_32-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Hale-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">\n        Chemistry\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Chemistry">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">\n        Rocket propellants\n       </a>\n       require a high\n       <a href="/wiki/Specific_energy" title="Specific energy">\n        specific energy\n       </a>\n       (energy per unit mass), because ideally all the reaction energy appears as kinetic energy of the exhaust gases, and exhaust velocity is the single most important performance parameter of an engine, on which vehicle performance depends.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Aside from inevitable losses and imperfections in the engine, incomplete combustion, etc., after specific reaction energy, the main theoretical limit reducing the exhaust velocity obtained is that, according to the laws of thermodynamics, a fraction of the chemical energy may go into rotation of the exhaust molecules, where it is unavailable for producing thrust. Monatomic gases like helium have only three degrees of freedom, corresponding to the three dimensions of space, {\n       <i>\n        x,y,z\n       </i>\n       }, and only such spherically symmetric molecules escape this kind of loss. A diatomic molecule like H\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       can rotate about either of the two axes perpendicular to the one joining the two atoms, and as the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equipartition" title="Equipartition">\n        equipartition\n       </a>\n       law of statistical mechanics demands that the available thermal energy be divided equally among the degrees of freedom, for such a gas in thermal equilibrium 3/5 of the energy can go into unidirectional motion, and 2/5 into rotation (actually, the vibration of the molecule should not be neglected, see\n       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity#Diatomic_gas" title="Heat capacity">\n        gas heat capacity\n       </a>\n       ). A triatomic molecule like water has six degrees of freedom, so the energy is divided equally among rotational and translational degrees of freedom. For most chemical reactions the latter situation is the case. This issue is traditionally described in terms of the ratio, gamma, of the specific heat of the gas at constant volume to that at constant pressure. The rotational energy loss is largely recovered in practice if the expansion nozzle is large enough to allow the gases to expand and cool sufficiently, the function of the nozzle being to convert the random thermal motions of the molecules in the combustion chamber into the unidirectional translation that produces thrust. As long as the exhaust gas remains in equilibrium as it expands, the initial rotational energy will be largely returned to translation in the nozzle.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Although the specific reaction energy per unit mass of reactants is key, low mean molecular weight in the reaction products is also important in practice in determining exhaust velocity. This is because the high gas temperatures in rocket engines pose serious problems for the engineering of survivable motors. Because temperature is proportional to the mean\n       <i>\n        energy per molecule\n       </i>\n       , a given amount of energy distributed among more molecules of lower mass permits a higher exhaust velocity at a given temperature. This means low atomic mass elements are favoured. Liquid\n       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">\n        hydrogen\n       </a>\n       (LH2) and\n       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">\n        oxygen\n       </a>\n       (LOX, or LO2), are the most effective propellants in terms of exhaust velocity that have been widely used to date, though a few exotic combinations involving boron or liquid ozone are potentially somewhat better in theory if various practical problems could be solved.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       It is important to note in computing the specific reaction energy, that the\n       <i>\n        entire mass of the propellants, including both fuel and oxidiser\n       </i>\n       , must be included. The fact that air-breathing engines are typically able to obtain oxygen "for free" without having to carry it along, accounts for one factor of why air-breathing engines are very much more propellant-mass efficient, and one reason that rocket engines are far less suitable for most ordinary terrestrial applications. Fuels for car or\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbojet_engine" title="Turbojet engine">\n        turbojet engines\n       </a>\n       , use atmospheric oxygen and so have a much better effective energy output per unit mass of propellant that must be carried, but are similar per unit mass of fuel.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Computer programs that predict the performance of propellants in rocket engines are available.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">\n        <a href="#cite_note-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">\n        <a href="#cite_note-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">\n        <a href="#cite_note-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ignition">\n        Ignition\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ignition">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Further information:\n       <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">\n        Combustion\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       With liquid and hybrid rockets, immediate ignition of the propellant(s) as they first enter the combustion chamber is essential.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       With liquid propellants (but not gaseous), failure to ignite within milliseconds usually causes too much liquid propellant to be inside the chamber, and if/when ignition occurs the amount of hot gas created can exceed the maximum design pressure of the chamber, causing a catastrophic failure of the pressure vessel. This is sometimes called a\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Hard_start" title="Hard start">\n         hard start\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       or a\n       <i>\n        rapid unscheduled disassembly\n       </i>\n       (RUD).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aw20121126_37-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-aw20121126-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Ignition can be achieved by a number of different methods; a pyrotechnic charge can be used, a plasma torch can be used,\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n       or electric spark ignition\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20161003_38-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-nsf20161003-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       may be employed. Some fuel/oxidiser combinations ignite on contact (\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypergolic" title="Hypergolic">\n        hypergolic\n       </a>\n       ), and non-hypergolic fuels can be "chemically ignited" by priming the fuel lines with hypergolic propellants (popular in Russian engines).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Gaseous propellants generally will not cause\n       <a href="/wiki/Hard_start" title="Hard start">\n        hard starts\n       </a>\n       , with rockets the total injector area is less than the throat thus the chamber pressure tends to ambient prior to ignition and high pressures cannot form even if the entire chamber is full of flammable gas at ignition.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Solid propellants are usually ignited with one-shot pyrotechnic devices.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutton_6-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sutton-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Once ignited, rocket chambers are self-sustaining and igniters are not needed. Indeed, chambers often spontaneously reignite if they are restarted after being shut down for a few seconds. However, when cooled, many rockets cannot be restarted without at least minor maintenance, such as replacement of the pyrotechnic igniter.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutton_6-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sutton-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Jet_physics">\n        Jet physics\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Jet physics">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1082" data-file-width="1514" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg/220px-Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg/330px-Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg/440px-Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Armadillo_Aerospace_Pixel_Hover.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Armadillo aerospace\'s quad vehicle showing visible banding (shock diamonds) in the exhaust Jet\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Rocket jets vary depending on the rocket engine, design altitude, altitude, thrust and other factors.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Carbon rich exhausts from\n       <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">\n        kerosene\n       </a>\n       fuels are often orange in colour due to the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_body_radiation" title="Black body radiation">\n        black body radiation\n       </a>\n       of the unburnt particles, in addition to the blue\n       <a href="/wiki/Swan_band" title="Swan band">\n        Swan bands\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_test_peroxide" title="High test peroxide">\n        Peroxide\n       </a>\n       oxidizer-based rockets and hydrogen rocket jets contain largely\n       <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">\n        steam\n       </a>\n       and are nearly invisible to the naked eye but shine brightly in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">\n        ultraviolet\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">\n        infrared\n       </a>\n       . Jets from\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_rocket" title="Solid rocket">\n        solid rockets\n       </a>\n       can be highly visible as the propellant frequently contains metals such as elemental aluminium which burns with an orange-white flame and adds energy to the combustion process.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Some exhausts, notably\n       <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">\n        alcohol\n       </a>\n       fuelled rockets, can show visible\n       <a href="/wiki/Shock_diamond" title="Shock diamond">\n        shock diamonds\n       </a>\n       . These are due to cyclic variations in the jet pressure relative to ambient creating shock waves that form \'Mach disks\'.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The shape of the jet varies from the design altitude, at high altitude all rockets are grossly under-expanded, and a quite small percentage of exhaust gases actually end up expanding forwards.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_rocket_engines">\n        Types of rocket engines\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Types of rocket engines">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physically_powered">\n        Physically powered\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Physically powered">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <table class="wikitable">\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Type\n         </b>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Description\n         </b>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Advantages\n         </b>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Disadvantages\n         </b>\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Water_rocket" title="Water rocket">\n          water rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Partially filled pressurised carbonated drinks container with tail and nose weighting\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Very simple to build\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Altitude typically limited to a few hundred feet or so (\n         <a href="/wiki/Water_rocket#Altitude_Record" title="Water rocket">\n          world record\n         </a>\n         is 623 meters/2,044 feet)\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Cold_gas_thruster" title="Cold gas thruster">\n          cold gas thruster\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         A non combusting form, used for\n         <a href="/wiki/Vernier_thruster" title="Vernier thruster">\n          vernier thrusters\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Non contaminating exhaust\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Extremely low performance\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemically_powered">\n        Chemically powered\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Chemically powered">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_rocket_propellant" title="Liquid rocket propellant">\n        Liquid rocket propellant\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid-fuel_rocket" title="Solid-fuel rocket">\n        solid-fuel rocket\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <table class="wikitable">\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Type\n         </b>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Description\n         </b>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Advantages\n         </b>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Disadvantages\n         </b>\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_rocket" title="Solid rocket">\n          Solid rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Ignitable, self-sustaining solid fuel/oxidiser mixture ("grain") with central hole and nozzle\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Simple, often no\n         <a href="/wiki/Moving_parts" title="Moving parts">\n          moving parts\n         </a>\n         , reasonably good mass fraction, reasonable\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_Impulse" title="Specific Impulse">\n          <i>\n           I\n          </i>\n          <sub>\n           sp\n          </sub>\n         </a>\n         . A thrust schedule can be designed into the grain.\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Throttling, burn termination, and reignition require special designs. Handling issues from ignitable mixture. Lower performance than liquid rockets. If grain cracks it can block nozzle with disastrous results. Grain cracks burn and widen during burn. Refueling harder than simply filling tanks.\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybrid_rocket" title="Hybrid rocket">\n          Hybrid rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Separate oxidiser/fuel; typically the oxidiser is liquid and kept in a tank and the fuel is solid.\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Quite simple, solid fuel is essentially inert without oxidiser, safer; cracks do not escalate, throttleable and easy to switch off.\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Some oxidisers are monopropellants, can explode in own right; mechanical failure of solid propellant can block nozzle (very rare with rubberised propellant), central hole widens over burn and negatively affects mixture ratio.\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">\n          Monopropellant rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Propellant (such as hydrazine, hydrogen peroxide or nitrous oxide) flows over a catalyst and exothermically decomposes; hot gases are emitted through nozzle.\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Simple in concept, throttleable, low temperatures in combustion chamber\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         catalysts can be easily contaminated, monopropellants can detonate if contaminated or provoked,\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_Impulse" title="Specific Impulse">\n          <i>\n           I\n          </i>\n          <sub>\n           sp\n          </sub>\n         </a>\n         is perhaps 1/3 of best liquids\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_bipropellant_rocket_engine" title="Liquid bipropellant rocket engine">\n          Bipropellant rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Two fluid (typically liquid) propellants are introduced through injectors into combustion chamber and burnt\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Up to ~99% efficient combustion with excellent mixture control, throttleable, can be used with turbopumps which permits incredibly lightweight tanks, can be safe with extreme care\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Pumps needed for high performance are expensive to design, huge thermal fluxes across combustion chamber wall can impact reuse, failure modes include major explosions, a lot of plumbing is needed.\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Dual_mode_propulsion_rocket" title="Dual mode propulsion rocket">\n          Dual mode propulsion rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Rocket takes off as a bipropellant rocket, then turns to using just one propellant as a monopropellant\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Simplicity and ease of control\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Lower performance than bipropellants\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Tripropellant_rocket" title="Tripropellant rocket">\n          Tripropellant rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Three different propellants (usually hydrogen, hydrocarbon and liquid oxygen) are introduced into a combustion chamber in variable mixture ratios, or multiple engines are used with fixed propellant mixture ratios and throttled or shut down\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Reduces take-off weight, since hydrogen is lighter; combines good thrust to weight with high average\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_Impulse" title="Specific Impulse">\n          <i>\n           I\n          </i>\n          <sub>\n           sp\n          </sub>\n         </a>\n         , improves payload for launching from Earth by a sizeable percentage\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Similar issues to bipropellant, but with more plumbing, more research and development\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Air-augmented_rocket" title="Air-augmented rocket">\n          Air-augmented rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Essentially a ramjet where intake air is compressed and burnt with the exhaust from a rocket\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Mach 0 to Mach 4.5+ (can also run exoatmospheric), good efficiency at Mach 2 to 4\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Similar efficiency to rockets at low speed or exoatmospheric, inlet difficulties, a relatively undeveloped and unexplored type, cooling difficulties, very noisy, thrust/weight ratio is similar to ramjets.\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turborocket" title="Turborocket">\n          Turborocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         A combined cycle turbojet/rocket where an additional oxidiser such as oxygen is added to the airstream to increase maximum altitude\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Very close to existing designs, operates in very high altitude, wide range of altitude and airspeed\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Atmospheric airspeed limited to same range as turbojet engine, carrying oxidiser like\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LOX" title="LOX">\n          LOX\n         </a>\n         can be dangerous. Much heavier than simple rockets.\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Precooled_jet_engine" title="Precooled jet engine">\n          Precooled jet engine\n         </a>\n         /\n         <a href="/wiki/Liquid_air_cycle_engine" title="Liquid air cycle engine">\n          LACE\n         </a>\n         (combined cycle with rocket)\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Intake air is chilled to very low temperatures at inlet before passing through a ramjet or turbojet engine. Can be combined with a rocket engine for orbital insertion.\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Easily tested on ground. High thrust/weight ratios are possible (~14) together with good fuel efficiency over a wide range of airspeeds, mach 0-5.5+; this combination of efficiencies may permit launching to orbit, single stage, or very rapid intercontinental travel.\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Exists only at the lab prototyping stage. Examples include\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RB545" title="RB545">\n          RB545\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_Engines_SABRE" title="Reaction Engines SABRE">\n          SABRE\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/ATREX" title="ATREX">\n          ATREX\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <table class="wikitable">\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n rocket">\n          Resistojet rocket\n         </a>\n         (electric heating)\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         A monopropellant is electrically heated by a filament for extra performance\n    1,600 seconds\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_Impulse" title="Specific Impulse">\n          <i>\n           I\n          </i>\n          <sub>\n           sp\n          </sub>\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Very low thrust and high power, performance is similar to\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_drive" title="Ion drive">\n          Ion drive\n         </a>\n         .\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Pulsed_plasma_thruster" title="Pulsed plasma thruster">\n          Pulsed plasma thruster\n         </a>\n         (electric arc heating; emits plasma)\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Plasma is used to erode a solid propellant\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         High\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_Impulse" title="Specific Impulse">\n          <i>\n           I\n          </i>\n          <sub>\n           sp\n          </sub>\n         </a>\n         , can be pulsed on and off for attitude control\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Low energetic efficiency\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Variable_specific_impulse_magnetoplasma_rocket" title="Variable specific impulse magnetoplasma rocket">\n          Variable specific impulse magnetoplasma rocket\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         Microwave heated plasma with magnetic throat/nozzle\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         Variable\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_Impulse" title="Specific Impulse">\n          <i>\n           I\n          </i>\n          <sub>\n           sp\n          </sub>\n         </a>\n         from 1,000 seconds to 10,000 seconds\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         similar thrust/weight ratio with ion drives (worse), thermal issues, as with ion drives very high power requirements for significant thrust, really needs advanced nuclear reactors, never flown, requires low temperatures for superconductors to work\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <div class="hatnote powered spacecraft propulsion\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal">\n        Thermal\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Thermal">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Preheated">\n        Preheated\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <table class="wikitable">\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n          Type\n         </b>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <b>\n
